Free Talk

Good Phenomenon 282

— Tang Tao

Because the National Games had athletes from the three northeastern provinces of Liaoning, Jilin, and Heilongjiang participating, some gentlemen felt relieved and considered it a good phenomenon.

In the past two years, quite a few so-called good phenomena have been discovered. The deeper the national crisis, the more good phenomena there seem to be. Shanghai has concessions, where living is like being in paradise (if you have money). National crises never come here, but good phenomena do exist.

Let's start from the beginning. Around the time the national crisis began, the Chinese in the concessions were quite enthusiastic for a while. They would run up to the top of tall buildings and shout towards the northeast, "Kill! Kill! Kill!" This was saving the country, a good phenomenon. Shops would put up signs saying, "This shop vows not to buy or sell enemy goods." Magazines and publications would publish articles about Japan. Students would lie down on train tracks with their lovers. There would be meetings and telegrams demanding military action. It was all very lively. It was all for saving the country, so who would dare say it wasn't a good phenomenon!

Once the fighting started in Zhabei, even people with scars on their bodies became patriotic fanatics. These were supposedly marks from being wounded by bullets, from fighting hand-to-hand with the Japanese soldiers, so that was also a good phenomenon. If someone was jilted in a love triangle, or too poor to live on, and they checked into a hotel to commit suicide, their suicide note would say, "Indignant at the unending foreign aggression, I commit suicide to repay the country." That was also a good phenomenon. Two years passed by in these good phenomena. Nowadays, you don't see as many articles about Japan in magazines and publications. The signs on shop doors have long been torn down, replaced by banners saying, "National goods sale!" The male and female students lying on the tracks had wedding banquets, and then quickly drafted divorce statements. The only meetings being held these days, besides the National Games, are probably fashion shows and other such grand events. What happened to everything? They say it's "saving the country, putting words into action after the shouting, good phenomena following good phenomena."

But scars are still scars, and suicides are no longer attributed to "repaying the country."

I don't want to mention the past, and I can temporarily not mention what's about to pass. If the creators of good phenomena are young, then they should still have a future.

On the Literary Anthology 283

— Chen Zizhan

Can the *Wen Xuan* anthology be recommended to modern youth as a must-read? If you ask the Sage of Poetry, he would say, "Become skilled and understand the principles of the *Wen Xuan*!" Unfortunately, there are no such sages of poetry to instruct our youth today. If you ask a literary giant, he would say, "If you've memorized the *Wen Xuan*, you're halfway to becoming a scholar." Unfortunately, there are no such literary giants serving as examiners to test our youth with such texts today. Has Heaven abandoned literature? Are the later generations not allowed to participate in it? We don't need to shake our heads or sigh. To cherish the "foundation of youth's literary cultivation," someone will naturally step forward and loudly proclaim the recommendation of this book to modern youth. There is no doubt about it. To read paralleled prose, you must read the *Wen Xuan*; to read prose, you must read *Zhuangzi*. Even without experienced elders, there are still exemplars. These two books are indeed exemplary works in ancient Chinese literature. Moreover, there are even young old souls who, when writing vernacular novels, open the *Wen Xuan* and "occasionally use words from the *Fu* on mountains and seas?" As for those who read such things and "find them stilted," they can only blame their own "deficiencies in the foundation of literary cultivation."

Speaking seriously, since we are discussing the *Wen Xuan*, we should briefly examine the book itself.

First, we should know that this book was compiled 1,400 years ago. Times have changed, and literature has also changed. If someone says that it is a book from the Qi and Liang dynasties, the "foundation of youth's literary cultivation," we would have no objection. But if someone considers it a must-read for modern youth in their literary cultivation, even though we might not say anything, the compiler of the book, Xiao Tong, if he had a spirit in the afterlife, would definitely issue a declaration and telegram to protest. Even if he didn't have a spirit in the afterlife, we can still examine his writings to see his views. Didn't he say in the preface to the *Wen Xuan* that literature should "change with the times"? Therefore, during the Qi and Liang dynasties, the era when paralleled prose was at its peak, he compiled this book with a focus on profound thought and elegant language, excluding even the texts of the classics, histories, and philosophers. If he were alive today, he might compile a new *Wen Xuan*. He certainly wouldn't create another book like the *Wen Xuan* to serve as the "foundation of youth's literary cultivation" because he knows that literature should "change with the times." He wouldn't tell people to go backwards!

Second, we should know that Xiao Tong was a "crown prince who, with his free time after official duties, spent many leisure days browsing the literary garden and widely perusing the forest of words." He compiled this *Wen Xuan* as a model reader for the aristocratic literature of that time. Today, what we call aristocratic literature, they called palatial style. It is said that the name palatial style was established by Xiao Gang. Because Xiao Tong died, he became the crown prince. He and Xu Chi and Yu Jianwu, the leaders of the writers serving the court, liked to innovate on old texts, not adhering to old styles. The writers serving the court at that time all learned from them, and the name palatial style arose from this. Xiao Gang himself also said, "I have had a passion for poetry since I was seven years old, and I never tire of it. However, I am prone to being frivolous and elegant, so it is called palatial style." We should know that Xiao Tong and Xiao Gang had the same views on literature, that literature should "change with the times," so they "liked to innovate" and opposed revivalism. Xiao Gang said, "I have never heard of composing poems to express emotions and character, yet imitating the *Inner Regulations*; wielding the pen to write intentions, yet copying the *Admonitions on Wine*. Dragging out the long spring days, yet studying the *Returning to the Source*; staring at the clear river water, yet imitating the *Great Treatise*." From this, we can roughly understand the nature of the *Wen Xuan* and the views of its compiler. Is it that we, living today, have no other books that can serve as the "foundation of youth's literary cultivation" except the *Wen Xuan*, which the aristocratic class considered fashionable 1,400 years ago?

Third, we should know that teaching people to "understand some methods of writing" by reading the *Wen Xuan* will make the skeptical and eccentric old man Zhuihui laugh his jaw off. Who can prove that the methods of writing of famous modern Chinese writers come from the *Wen Xuan*? The skeptical and eccentric old man Tong once cursed the *Wen Xuan* as a "literary monster." If he saw the *Wen Xuan*, he might throw a sulfur fire at it. The old man Zhuihui writes with the spirit of "fart, fart, what utter nonsense," which he says he learned from the *He Dian*. If he saw the *Wen Xuan*, he might throw it into the latrine. Moreover, he once advocated throwing all Chinese thread-bound books into the latrine for 30 years before discussing them again! As for saying that reading the *Wen Xuan* "can expand one's vocabulary," that is not entirely true. Besides the dead words, how many living words can be transplanted into the modern writing of modern people? Besides the ancient styles of the old guard, who has seen how many words in modern writing come from the *Wen Xuan*? And are these words fresh and lively? Are modern writers all "too clumsy and straightforward in their writing, with too small a vocabulary"?

To speak very "clumsily and straightforwardly," I have unknowingly become too long-winded. I will "stop here."

Account of the Games 284

— Gao Zhi

The venue for the National Games was south of the Sun Yat-sen Mausoleum. From the Daxing Palace in the city, there were direct buses from the Jiangnan Long-Distance Bus Company to the main gate of the stadium. The first day was Double Ten Day, and there were too many people wanting to see the games. In order to grab seats on the buses, they smashed over ten of them. The lack of orderliness surpassed even that at the train station and docks. The staff at the bus company went on strike, so going was out of the question. Later, when the buses resumed service, I decided to go and have a look.

Getting on the bus was a squeeze, with many white-haired old men also (pushing) forward. Sitting on the bus was also a squeeze. Since we had squeezed onto the bus, some people didn't even need to buy tickets. In Nanjing dialect, this is called "mooching," and those who were not shy about mooching were the ones wearing suits. Getting off the bus and buying entrance tickets was a squeeze, but mooching and crowding (went hand in hand). Many people rushed into the stadium through the iron gates without buying tickets. Inside the stadium, it was still crowded. If one were to say that "disorderliness" is one of the national essences, it probably wouldn't be wrong, as it was evident everywhere.

As I sat down in the stands, I heard the announcer on the loudspeaker telling people in the field to leave and not obstruct the games. However, those wives and young mistresses kept walking back and forth on the field. So the loudspeaker kept shouting, "(Idle people, stand outside the field!)"

People were constantly running around in the stands, some even bringing bread and water bottles into the stadium, combining watching the games with a picnic. As a result, there was water and trash on the ground. Sometimes a group of people would run here and squeeze there. While running and squeezing, their feet didn't care about anything and left many muddy footprints on the clean seats. So the audience behind would shout at those in front, "(Sit down! Sit down!)"

Soldiers were indispensable for maintaining order in the stadium. If there were soldiers in the stands, it would be a large group. But conflicts often arose with the audience. The audience only heard many soldiers shouting loudly, "(Beat them! Beat them!)" The spirit of soldiers fighting battles was on full display at the stadium.

The women at the stadium were all dressed up in bright and colorful clothes. All kinds of (bizarre outfits) that were prohibited by the Ministry of Interior were here. This spontaneous fashion show was indeed more interesting than a few famous socialites and young ladies temporarily wearing a few fashionable domestically produced clothes and turning around on stage. It's not easy to see so many (high-class women) normally, but at the stadium, you could see many men with great airs walking around with their daughter-like or granddaughter-like young lovers. And many (unrelated) women were walking around on the sports field. Many non-(high-class) people came to see the people and the spectacle, like visiting the Temple of the City God. They were the best at crowding, and sometimes the Boy Scouts had to shout loudly, "(Those with strength, squeeze the men, don't squeeze the women)."

With many people and few buses, everyone started crowding in front of the bus station from 4 p.m. onwards, scrambling to buy tickets. Crowding and more crowding. I really didn't dare (push forward) and had to take a large truck, being loaded into the city like cargo. There were more police, and each bus had (armed comrades) escorting it, which surprisingly had (unexpected effectiveness), and no more vehicles were damaged.

Thinking about the issue of national essence, I feel that (disorderliness) is unavoidable.

Correction 285

In yesterday's article "(A Point of Dissent)," the last sentence was "(I am ashamed of it)." The character (荍) was mistakenly printed as (獲). Also, in Mr. Shi Zhicun's letter to me, there was a place name (王家厙). Because I had walked in that area and seen a sign for (王家沙), I changed the character (厙) to (沙). Now someone says it should actually be (厙), and this is hereby corrected.

(Liewen)

Two Brothers (4) 286

— By Maupassant, Translated by Li Weike

Mrs. Luc was a fair-haired woman of angular figure, so light that a mere breath of wind seemed sufficient to carry her away. Her small face seemed determined, brave, and combative, a contrast to her naturally refined disposition.

By reason of their similarity of temperament, she had early taken a liking to Jean. But this preference was only expressed in a barely perceptible difference of tone and glance, and sometimes when she accepted his opinions; even then it was only visible in her expression. She seemed to have guessed that Jean’s opinions would only serve to make her own the more firm, while Pierre’s would inevitably be different from hers. When she spoke of the doctor's ideas, his politics, art, philosophy, ethics, and so forth, she would sometimes say, "(You and your nonsensical ideas.)" Whereupon, he would regard her with the cold glance of a magistrate, as if he were examining a woman's lawsuit, the lawsuit of all women, poor creatures that they are!

Old Roland had never asked her to go fishing with him when his two sons were not at home, and he had never even taken his own wife, for he liked to start before dawn with Captain Bocquet and old Papagris the sailor. Captain Bocquet was a retired mariner whom he had met on the quay at high tide, and who had become a close friend. Old Papagris the sailor had the nickname Jean Bart (1650–1702, a famous pirate), and was employed by him to look after his boat.

But one evening last week, at dinner at his house, Mrs. Luc had said to him, "(Fishing must be a very interesting sport, is it not?)" Old Roland, who had been a jeweler, on hearing this, became very delighted and wished to make her share his own passion, as a missionary desires to gain converts, exclaiming, "(Will you go with us?)" "(Certainly.)" "(Shall we say next Tuesday?)" "(Yes, next Tuesday.)" "(But can you start at five o'clock in the morning?)" She gave a cry of alarm, "(Oh, no! Not so early.)"

He was disappointed and became cold, and at once suspected that she had no real interest in the matter. But he still asked, "(At what time can you start then?)" "(Why... at nine o'clock, perhaps!)" "(Cannot it be earlier?)" "(No, it cannot be earlier; that is quite early enough!)" The good man hesitated. So late a start would be sure to lead to nothing, for if the sun became hot the fish would no longer bite. But the two brothers hastened to smooth matters over and quickly arranged and decided everything. Thus, on the following Tuesday, Old Roland's fishing boat, the Pearl, dropped anchor at the foot of the rocky coast of Heve harbor. They fished until midday, rested for a while, and then fished again; but they caught nothing. Old Roland then knew that Mrs. Luc only cared for a sail on the sea, and did not care for fishing, and seeing that all the fishing rods remained motionless, he uttered, in an unreasonable fit of annoyance, a loud "Phew!" This long-drawn "Phew!" was directed at the indifferent widow and the unattainable fishes alike.
